1558554331559	u)-DePM,Z}	1506525119072	Why are government bond markets usually more efficient than property markets?	Government bond markets have more freely available information and lower transaction costs.<div><br></div><div>Therefore, it is easier to spot mispricings and easier to exploit them when found. This raises the desirability of underpriced securities, and lowers the desirability of overpriced ones.</div><div><br></div><div>The result is that mispricings are more likely to (and quicker to) disappear.</div>			<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 1 p. 7

1558775411176	D9.OK;/<XP	1562101354843	<div>The axioms required for the expected utility theorem are as follows.<br><br>Let \(\Omega\) be a sample space with \(\sigma\)-algebra \(\mathcal{F}\) and 'preference relation', \(\preccurlyeq \subseteq \Omega^2\), such that</div><div><br></div>1) {{c1::\(\preccurlyeq\) is both<div>&nbsp; &nbsp;a) <u>Connex</u> (sometimes called comparability of outcomes). That is, if \(A \preccurlyeq B \preccurlyeq C\) then \(A \preccurlyeq C\) for every \(A, B, C \in \Omega\).</div></div>}}<div><div><br></div><div>Further, extend \(\preccurlyeq\), to a relation \(\preccurlyeq^\prime \in {\Omega^\prime}^2\), where \(\Omega^\prime\) is the set of all probability spaces over \(\Omega\) where</div></div><div><br></div><div>2) {{c2::if \(A \approx B\) and \(\alpha, \beta \in \Omega^\prime\) such that \(\mathbb{P}_\alpha(C) = \mathbb{P}_\beta(C)\) for every \(A \neq C \neq B\) and \(\mathbb{P}_\alpha(A) = \mathbb{P}_\beta(B)\) then \(\alpha \approx^\prime \beta\). (This is called i<u>ndependence</u>&nbsp;– it essentially says that gambles are equally preferable if all individual outcomes have the same utility.<u>)</u>}}</div><div><br></div><div>and</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c3::if \(C \preccurlyeq B \preccurlyeq A\) then there exists \(\alpha \in \Omega^\prime\) such that \(\alpha \approx^\prime \beta\) where \(\beta\) is defined by \(\mathbb{P}_\beta(B) = 1\). (This is called <u>certainty equivalence</u>&nbsp;– it essentially says that for any good and bad outcomes \(A\) and \(C\), it's possible to make the probability of the good one low enough and the bad one high enough, that the utility of the gamble equals that of any given outcome between \(A\) and \(C\).)}}</div>	"<img src=""Utility theory.svg""><br>"		<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 2 p. 7		
1558776940469	Ky}$qpEx&2	1506525119072	State the <u>expected utility <i>theorem</i></u>.	Given the axioms of the expected utility theorem, we have the following.<div><br></div><div>1) There exists a utility function \(U: \Omega^\prime \to \mathbb{R}\) such that \[U(A) \leq U(B) \Leftrightarrow A \preccurlyeq B.\]<div><br></div><div>2) For any pair of gambles, \(\alpha, \beta \in \Omega^\prime\) and any such function, we have \[\mathbb{E}_\alpha(U) \leq \mathbb{E}_\beta(U) \Leftrightarrow \alpha \preccurlyeq^\prime \beta.\]</div><div>In particular when \(\Omega^\prime\) is finite, \[\sup{}_{\preccurlyeq^\prime} \Omega^\prime = \alpha \Leftrightarrow \mathbb{E}_\alpha(U) = \max\{\mathbb{E}_\beta(U)\}_{\beta \in \Omega^\prime}.\]</div></div>	"<img src=""Utility theory.svg""><br>"		<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 2 p. 4.

In reality, investors do not dislike uncertainty of returns, but the possibility of loss. For example, '10% gain for certain' is more certain than '10% or 15% gain with 1/2 probability', but the latter is clearly preferable.<div><br></div><div>Because of this, the variance of returns may not be appropriate because it penalises all forms of uncertainly equally.</div>	"<img src=""Measures of risk (2).svg""><br>"		<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 4 p. 6

1563359928560	PO.%z4u?8	1562101354843	{{c6::Ito's lemma}} states the following.<br><br>Let \(f\) be an Ito function and \(X_t\) be an Ito process where \(X_t = \mu(X_t, t)\textrm{d}t + \sigma(X_t, t)\textrm{d}W_t\). Then \[\begin{align}f(X_T, T) &amp; = {{c1::f(X_0, 0)}}\\ &amp; + \int_0^T\left({{c2::\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} }} + {{c3::\mu \frac{\partial f}{\partial x} }} + {{c4::\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x^2} }}\right)(X_t, t)\ \textrm{d}t\\ &amp; + \int_0^T \left({{c5::\sigma\frac{\partial f}{\partial x} }}\right)(X_t, t)\ \textrm{d}W_t.\end{align}\] Equivalently in shorthand form that is \[\begin{align}\textrm{d}f &amp; = \left({{c2::\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} }} + {{c3::\mu \frac{\partial f}{\partial x} }} + {{c4::\frac{1}{2}\sigma^2 \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial x^2} }}\right)\ \textrm{d}t\\ &amp; + \left({{c5::\sigma\frac{\partial f}{\partial x} }}\right)\ \textrm{d}W_t.\end{align}\]	Note that something like \(\frac{\partial f}{\partial X_t}\) is technically incorrect. To apply Ito's lemma we compute the partial derivatives of the deterministic function \(f(x,t)\) and evaluate these at the random point \((X_t, t)\). That is, \(\frac{\partial f}{\partial x}(X_t, t)\).		<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 10 p. 15		
1563361889694	ig^Z9B$2Od	1562101354843	The Black–Scholes PDE is derived as follows.<br><br>Let \(f(t, S_t)\) be the price of a derivative at time \(t\) of an underlying with price \(S_t\).<br><div><br></div><div>1) {{c1::We contruct a risk-free portfolio with<br>&nbsp; &nbsp; • minus 1 holding of \(f\),<br>&nbsp; &nbsp; • \(\frac{\partial f}{\partial s}\) in shares of \(S_t\)<br>That is, \[V(t, S_t) = -f(t, S_t) + \frac{\partial f}{\partial s} S_t.\]}}<br>2) {{c2::The instantaneous investment gain for \(V\) is \[-\textrm{d}f + \frac{\partial f}{\partial s} \textrm{d}S_t.\]}}</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c3::<u>Applying Ito's lemma</u> to \(f\), the expressions with \(Z_t\) cancel and we end up with \[-\textrm{d}f + \frac{\partial f}{\partial s} \textrm{d}S_t = \left(-\frac{\partial f}{\partial t} - \frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial^2 f}{\partial s^2}\sigma^2 S_t^2 \right) \textrm{d}t.\]}}</div><div><br></div><div>4) {{c4::Since the RHS does not involve \(\textrm{d}Z_t\), it involves no randomness over a short interval, the investment gain over a short interval must be risk-free. Hence, by the principle of no arbitrage, the investment gain is equal to \(rV(t, S_t)\) where \(r\) is the risk-free rate.}}</div><div><br></div><div>5) {{c5::Equating these two expressions for the investment gain yields the Black–Scholes PDE.}}</div>			<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 15 p. 10

1563383482778	DX].bN8VON	1562101354843	The 5-step method is {{c1::a process for proving the formula for the fair price of a derivative}}. It proceeds as follows.<div><br></div><div>1) {{c2::Let \(Q\) be the risk-neutral probability measure with \(P \sim Q\). That is, so that \(D_t = e^{-rt}S_t\) is martingale. Derive \(Q\) based on the distribution of \(S_t\).}}</div><div><br></div><div>2) {{c3::Let \(V_t = e^{-r(n-t)} \mathbb{E}_Q(C_n\mid \mathcal{F}_t)\), where \(C_n\) is the derivative payoff at time \(n\). (We aim to show that this is the fair price / a replicating profolio.)}}</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c4::Let \(E_t = B_n^{-1}\mathbb{E}_Q(C_n\mid \mathcal{F}_t) = e^{-rt}V_t\). That is, let \(E_t\) be a discounted version of the replicating porfolio \(V_t\).}}</div><div><br></div><div>3b) {{c5::Show that \(E_t\) is martingale with respect to \(Q\) (using the tower property of expectation).}}</div><div><br></div><div>4) {{c6::By the martingale representation theorem we have \(\textrm{d} E_t = \phi_t \textrm{d}D_t\) for \(\phi_t\) which can be determined uniquely.}}</div><div><br></div><div>5) {{c7::Let \(\psi_t = E_{t} - \phi_t D_{t}\). Show that the portfolio \((\phi_t, \psi_t)\) is equal in value to \(V_t\), is self-financing, and has a value of \(C_n\) at time \(n\). Hence \(V_t\) is the value of a replicating portfolio of the derivative.}}</div>			<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), chp. 16 p. 19

1567938857565	ca6]#yUVI9	1508676822151	Explain how the market price of risk can be used to transform a pricing SDE from the real-world to the risk-neutral probability measure. This is the standard Brownian motion under \(Q\).<div><br></div><div>This can then be sustituted into the original SDE (replacing \(W_t\) with \(\tilde{W}_t - \gamma(t)\)). This is all that is required.</div>			<i>CM2.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2019), September 2014 exam, q. 5

1578912408405	tUf9Z>?+&z	1562101354843	The 'key 14 steps' for computational actuarial modelling are:<div><br></div><div>1) {{c1::define the objectives to be met by the modelling process;}}</div><div><br></div><div>2) {{c2::plan the modelling process and how the model will be validated;}}</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c3::collect and analyse the data for the model;}}</div><div><br></div><div>4) {{c4::define the parameters for the model and consider appropriate parameter values;}}</div><div><br></div><div>5) {{c5::capture the essence of the real-world system in a (relatively) simple initial model;}}</div><div><br></div><div>6) {{c6::ask for experts on the real-world system for input;}}</div><div><br></div><div>7) {{c7::decide if a simulation package or a general-purpose language is appropriate, and if applicable choose an appropriate random number generator;}}</div><div><br></div><div>8) {{c8::write the computer program for the model;}}</div><div><br></div><div>9) {{c9::debug the program / check it is working as intended;}}</div><div><br></div><div>10) {{c10::check the reasonableness of the model output;}}</div><div><br></div><div>11) {{c11::review the appropriateness of the model with respect to small changes in the input parameters;}}</div><div><br></div><div>12) {{c12::analyse the output of the model;}}</div><div><br></div><div>13) {{c13::check that the applicable technical guidance has been complied with (e.g. TAS100);}}</div><div><br></div><div>14) {{c14::communicate and document the results and the model.}}</div>			<i>CM1.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2020), chp. 1 p. 19		
1578912939723	E?}y~ijFgn	1562101354843	The advantages of computational models include:<div><br></div><div>1) {{c1::being able to study long-run phenomena in compressed time;}}</div><div><br></div><div>2) {{c2::the results of a model can be easier to interpret than a mathematical / logical model of the same system;}}</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c3::a model allows one to 'try out' different courses of action when to do so in reality would be costly or impossible.}}</div>			<i>CM1.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2020), chp. 1 p. 8		
1578913221438	h&R.a+d+Ox	1562101354843	The disadvantages of computational models include:<div><br></div><div>1) {{c1::developing a model can require considerable investment of time and/or expertise;}}</div><div><br></div><div>2) {{c2::stochastic models may need to be run many times since the outputs are only estimates;}}</div><div><br></div><div>3) {{c3::it can be very difficult to use a model to find a required set of inputs for a particular outcome (compared to predicting an outcome based on a particular input);}}</div><div><br></div><div>4) {{c4::it can produce a false sense of confidence, especially if the model has not been validated;}}</div><div><br></div><div>5) {{c5::models rely heavily on data input, meaning that bad data will produce bad output;}}</div><div><br></div><div>6) {{c6::there is potential that a model will be misused, particularly in the case when the users of a model are not aware of it's limitations or how it works;}}</div><div><br></div><div>7) {{c7::no model can incorporate all possible eventualities (e.g. new legislation);}}</div><div><br></div><div>8) {{c8::the model outputs may be difficult to interpret.}}</div>			<i>CM1.</i>&nbsp;ActEd Combined Materials Pack (2020), chp. 1 p. 8

1631793634232	k*)Cyz=O8q	1508676822151	Consider the following categorical observation, along with the expected observation under the null hypothesis. \[\begin{array}&amp; &amp; \textrm{Obs.} &amp; \textrm{Exp.} \\ A &amp; 96 &amp; 75\\ B &amp; 41 &amp; 59\\ C &amp; 60 &amp; 62\end{array}\] Describe how a chi-squared test would be performed to test the null hypothesis in this case.	First we calculate the individual standardised diviations for each observation–expectation pair: \(z_i = \frac{O_i-E_i}{\sqrt{E_i}}\), where \(O_i\)&nbsp; and \(E_i\) are the i-th observed and expected value respectively.<br><br>Assuming that the central limit theorem holds for this data, each diviation will have (asymptotically) \(z_i \sim \mathcal{N}(0,1)\).<br><br>Therefore \(\sum_{i=1}^n z_i^2 \sim \chi_n^2\), and from here a \(p\)-value can be calculated in the usual way.<br><br>In this case we get a \(p\)-value of approximately 1%. (Using 3 degrees of freedom.) Note that e.g. if the categories are exhaustive then they are not independent, so the degrees of freedom would need to be reduced by 1.
